## Your 11,000 qBraid Credits — How to Use Them

### What's Available on qBraid

**GPU Instances** (for training + CUDA-Q simulation):

| Instance | GPU | Credits/min | Credits/hour | ~Hours with 11,000 cr |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `gpu-l40s` | 1x L40S 48GB | 3.80 | 228 | ~48 hours |
| `gpu-h100-sxm` | 1x H100 80GB | 8.95 | 537 | ~20 hours |
| `gpu-a100-sxm` | 1x A100 80GB | 4.15 | 249 | ~44 hours |
| `gpu-gh200` | 1x GH200 96GB | 4.78 | 287 | ~38 hours |
| `gpu-rtx-4090` | 1x RTX 4090 24GB | 1.45 | 87 | ~126 hours |

**QPU Access** (for running generated circuits on real hardware):

| QPU | Provider | Per-task | Per-shot | Max qubits | Feasible for us? |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| **IonQ Forte-1** | IonQ | 30 cr | 8 cr | ~30q | ✅ Best for 4-12q molecules |
| **IonQ Forte-Enterprise-1** | IonQ | 30 cr | 8 cr | ~30q | ✅ Same |
| **IQM Emerald** | AWS | 30 cr | 0.16 cr | 54q | ✅ Cheapest per-shot |
| **IQM Garnet** | AWS | 30 cr | 0.145 cr | 20q | ✅ Good for 8-12q |
| **Rigetti Cepheus-1-108Q** | AWS | 30 cr | 0.0425 cr | 108q | ✅ Cheapest shots, most qubits |
| **AQT IBEX Q1** | AQT | 30 cr | 2.35 cr | ~24q | ⚠️ Expensive shots |
| **QuEra Aquila** | QuEra | 30 cr | 1 cr | 256q (neutral atom) | ⚠️ Analog Hamiltonian, not gate-based |

**Free simulator**: `qbraid:qbraid:sim:qir-sv` — up to 30 qubits, 2000 shots, **free** (no credits)

### Recommended Strategy for 11,000 Credits

**Priority 1: qBraid GPU for RL training + reproducibility (Phase 3 requirement)**

The competition requires judges to reproduce results on qBraid. Use a qBraid GPU instance to:
- Run the full [run_gic2026_scaling.sh](file:///scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials/scripts/run_gic2026_scaling.sh:0:0-0:0) pipeline
- Verify our code works in qBraid Lab out-of-the-box
- Generate reproducible results

| Task | Instance | Est. credits | Est. time |
|---|---|---|---|
| Full pipeline run (Ham + RL + inference + Stage 2 + GQE baseline) | `gpu-l40s` (1x L40S) | ~2,000 cr | ~8 hours |
| RL training only (Chemeleon2, 500 epochs) | `gpu-l40s` | ~1,500 cr | ~6 hours |
| 40+ qubit MPS scaling benchmark | `gpu-h100-sxm` (80GB for MPS) | ~500 cr | ~1 hour |

**Priority 2: QPU execution on real hardware (bonus points + strong Phase 3 execution)**

Our GQE circuits are shallow (10-20 Pauli rotations) and use 4-12 qubits for the small molecules. The cheapest path:

| Molecule | Qubits | QPU | Shots needed | Est. cost |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| H2 | 4 | IQM Garnet | 1000 × 15 terms = 15k shots | 30 + 2,175 = ~2,205 cr |
| LiH (active space) | 8 | IQM Garnet | 1000 × 185 terms = 185k shots | 30 + 26,825 = too expensive |
| H2 | 4 | Rigetti Cepheus | 1000 × 15 terms = 15k shots | 30 + 638 = ~668 cr |
| H2 | 4 | IonQ Forte-1 | 1000 × 15 terms = 15k shots | 30 + 120,000 = too expensive |

**Best QPU plan**: Run H2 (4q) on **Rigetti Cepheus-1-108Q** — cheapest per-shot at 0.0425 cr/shot. With 15 Pauli terms × 1024 shots = 15,360 shots → ~653 cr + 30 cr/task = **~683 credits**. This gives us a real hardware energy for H2, which is a massive Phase 3 differentiator.

**Priority 3: Free qBraid simulator for reproducibility validation**

Use `qbraid:qbraid:sim:qir-sv` (free, up to 30q) to validate all our circuits run correctly on qBraid's infrastructure. This costs zero credits and satisfies the "judges can re-run" requirement.

### Budget Breakdown (11,000 credits)

| Activity | Credits | What it gets us |
|---|---|---|
| qBraid GPU pipeline run (L40S, 8h) | ~2,000 | Reproducible results on qBraid |
| H100 MPS benchmark (1h) | ~500 | 40+ qubit scaling data |
| H2 on Rigetti Cepheus QPU | ~700 | Real quantum hardware energy |
| LiH on Rigetti (reduced shots, 100/term) | ~1,170 | 8q on real hardware |
| Free qBraid simulator validation | 0 | Reproducibility for judges |
| **Total** | **~4,370** | Leaves ~6,630 for iteration |

---

## qBraid V2 Documentation — Key Takeaways for Conditional-GQE

### Platform Architecture

qBraid V2 has 6 main sections: **Lab** (JupyterLab cloud), **VS Code** (cloud IDE), **AI** (agent mode + chat), **SDK** (Python runtime), **CLI** (command-line), and **APIs** (REST).

### 1. qBraid Lab (Cloud Environment)

- **On-Demand GPU Instances**: Launch from dashboard → On-Demand tab. Billed per minute in credits. 3 access modes: JupyterLab, VS Code, Terminal.
- **Lifecycle**: `Launch → Running → Stop → Stopped → Resume → Running`. Terminate deletes everything.
- **File Persistence**: On-demand instances have independent disks — no auto-sync with cloud storage. Stop preserves files, terminate deletes them. Must manually download from Files tab.
- **Concurrent instances**: 100+ credits = 1, 1,000+ = 2, 2,500+ = 5 max concurrent.
- **GPU image**: Pre-configured with NVIDIA cuQuantum SDK, CUDA-Q, Qiskit, Cirq, and other quantum SDKs out of the box.

### 2. qBraid SDK (Python Runtime)

**QbraidProvider** — the main interface:
```python
from qbraid import QbraidProvider
provider = QbraidProvider()
devices = provider.get_devices()  # list all available
device = provider.get_device("aws:rigetti:qpu:cepheus-1-108q")
job = device.run(circuit, shots=1024)
result = job.result()
counts = result.data.get_counts()
```

**Key features**:
- **18+ program types supported**: Qiskit, Cirq, Braket, PennyLane, PyQuil, pytket, OpenQASM 2/3, pyqir, CUDA-Q, Qibo, stim, Pulser, and more
- **Transpiler**: `qbraid.transpile(circuit, 'openqasm3')` converts between any supported formats
- **Transforms**: `program.transform(device)` adapts circuits to target hardware basis gates
- **Batch jobs**: `device.run([circ1, circ2, circ3], shots=1000, as_batch=True)` → single job, `BatchResult` with per-circuit results and error handling
- **Group jobs**: Context manager for submitting across multiple devices simultaneously
- **Async results**: `result = await job.async_result(timeout=120, poll_interval=5)`

**Runtime options** (provider-specific):
- **Rigetti (AWS)**: `{"disable_qubit_rewiring": True}` 
- **IonQ**: `{"noise": {"model": "aria-1", "seed": 42}}` or `{"noise": "ideal"}`
- **Azure/Quantinuum**: `{"simulator": "stabilizer"}`, `{"error-model": False, "no-opt": True}`

### 3. Available Devices (Full List)

**QPUs** (20 devices across 7 providers):
| Device ID | Provider | Type |
|---|---|---|
| `aws:rigetti:qpu:cepheus-1-108q` | Rigetti (AWS) | Superconducting, 108q |
| `aws:iqm:qpu:emerald` | IQM (AWS) | Superconducting, 54q |
| `aws:iqm:qpu:garnet` | IQM (AWS) | Superconducting, 20q |
| `aws:ionq:qpu:forte-1` | IonQ (AWS) | Trapped ion, ~30q |
| `aws:ionq:qpu:forte-enterprise-1` | IonQ (AWS) | Trapped ion, ~30q |
| `aws:aqt:qpu:ibex-q1` | AQT (AWS) | Trapped ion, ~24q |
| `aws:quera:qpu:aquila` | QuEra (AWS) | Neutral atom, 256q (analog) |
| `azure:ionq:qpu:forte-1` | IonQ (Azure) | Trapped ion |
| `azure:ionq:qpu:forte-enterprise-1` | IonQ (Azure) | Trapped ion |
| `azure:pasqal:qpu:fresnel` | Pasqal (Azure) | Neutral atom |
| `azure:rigetti:qpu:cepheus-1-36q` | Rigetti (Azure) | 36q |
| `rigetti:rigetti:qpu:cepheus-1-108q` | Rigetti (Direct) | 108q, per-minute billing |

**Simulators** (12 devices):
| Device ID | Type | Max qubits |
|---|---|---|
| `qbraid:qbraid:sim:qir-sv` | qBraid QIR state-vector | 30q (free) |
| `ionq:ionq:sim:simulator` | IonQ simulator | — |
| `aws:aws:sim:sv1` | AWS state vector | — |
| `aws:aws:sim:tn1` | AWS tensor network | Large systems |
| `aws:aws:sim:dm1` | AWS density matrix | Noise simulation |
| `azure:quantinuum:sim:h2-1e` | Quantinuum H2 emulator | — |
| `azure:quantinuum:sim:h2-1sc` | Quantinuum H2 syntax checker | — |
| `azure:pasqal:sim:emu-tn` | Pasqal tensor network emulator | — |

### 4. CUDA-Q + qBraid Integration

qBraid is now a **native CUDA-Q cloud target** (June 2026):
```bash
nvq++ --target qbraid kernel.cpp -o kernel
export QBRAID_API_KEY=<key>
# Default: free state-vector simulator
# For QPU: --qbraid-machine aws:rigetti:qpu:cepheus-1-108q
```
- Same CUDA-Q kernel code runs on simulators and QPUs — only the machine flag changes
- No `--emulate` flag support (all jobs execute on qBraid cloud)
- Default backend: `qbraid:qbraid:sim:qir-sv` (free, 30q, 2000 shots)

### 5. qBraid CLI

```bash
qbraid compute up gpu-l40s          # Launch GPU instance
qbraid devices list --type QPU      # List available QPUs
qbraid jobs submit <circuit> <device>  # Submit job
qbraid envs list                    # List Python environments
qbraid skills create                # Create AI coding skill
```

### 6. What This Means for Our [qbraid_backend.py](file:///scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials/src/gqe/eval/qbraid_backend.py:0:0-0:0)

Our existing code at `@/scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials/src/gqe/eval/qbraid_backend.py` already uses `QbraidProvider` correctly. Key improvements we could make based on the docs:

1. **Batch jobs**: Instead of measuring each Hamiltonian term separately (15+ separate QPU jobs for H2), submit all measurement circuits as a single batch: `device.run([meas_circ_1, meas_circ_2, ...], shots=1024, as_batch=True)` — this is a single QPU task, saving 14× per-task fees (30 cr each = 420 cr saved for H2)

2. **Transforms**: Use `program.transform(device)` to automatically adapt our Qiskit circuits to each QPU's native gate set (e.g., Rigetti's CZ/XY gates, IonQ's GPI/GPI2/MS gates)

3. **CUDA-Q native path**: Instead of translating to Qiskit, we could compile our CUDA-Q kernels directly with `nvq++ --target qbraid --qbraid-machine aws:rigetti:qpu:cepheus-1-108q` — zero code changes to our existing CUDA-Q kernel code

4. **Free simulator for validation**: `qbraid:qbraid:sim:qir-sv` at 30 qubits covers all our molecules up to N2 (20q) and BeH2 (14q) — zero cost reproducibility

The **batch job** approach is the biggest win — it would reduce H2 QPU cost from ~683 credits (15 separate tasks) to ~53 credits (1 batch task + shots). For LiH (185 terms), that's 184 × 30 = 5,520 credits saved.

# How to Use qBraid for Your GQE Project — Complete Practical Guide

## The Big Picture

Your repo (`Quantum-Buddies/Conditional_GQE`) already has CUDA-Q kernels, a qBraid backend script, and a requirements file. qBraid is now a **native CUDA-Q cloud target** — meaning your existing CUDA-Q Python code can run on qBraid's QPUs and simulators with **zero code changes**, just a different `set_target()` call. Here's how to put it all together:

---

## Step 1: Prepare the GitHub Repo (with Git LFS)

You already pushed to `Conditional_GQE`. Now add LFS for large binary artifacts so judges can clone everything:

```bash
cd /scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials

# Initialize Git LFS
git lfs install

# Track large files (>50MB) that can't go in regular git
git lfs track "*.pth"          # model checkpoints
git lfs track "*.pt"           # PyTorch checkpoints
git lfs track "results/data/hamiltonians_40plus.json/**"
git lfs track "results/data/hamiltonians_scaling.json/**"
git lfs track "results/scaling_benchmark/**/*.npy"
git lfs track "results/scaling_benchmark/**/*.pt"

# Add and commit the LFS rules
git add .gitattributes
git commit -m "chore: add Git LFS tracking for checkpoints and large Hamiltonian JSONs"

# Add the large files themselves
git add results/data/hamiltonians_40plus.json/ results/data/hamiltonians_scaling.json/
git add results/scaling_benchmark/  # if any exist
git commit -m "data: add large Hamiltonian datasets and scaling benchmarks via LFS"

git push origin main
```

| What to put in Git LFS | What to keep in regular Git |
|---|---|
| `*.pth` / `*.pt` checkpoints | All `.py` source code |
| Large Hamiltonian JSONs (>50MB) | Configs (`.yaml`, `.yml`) |
| `*.npy` benchmark arrays | Shell scripts (`.sh`) |
| PDF reports | [requirements-qbraid.txt](file:///scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials/requirements-qbraid.txt:0:0-0:0) |
| Plot PNGs (>5MB) | [README.md](file:///scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials/README.md:0:0-0:0), `docs/` |

## Step 2: Launch a qBraid GPU Instance

From [qBraid Lab](https://account.qbraid.com/dashboard) → On-Demand tab, or via CLI:

```bash
qbraid compute up gpu-l40s
```

### GPU Instance Selection Guide

| Your Task | Instance | VRAM | Credits/min | Credits/hour | Why |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| **RL training (DAPO)** | `gpu-l40s` | 48GB | 3.80 | 228 | Matches AIRE L40S, cheapest per GB |
| **40+ qubit MPS simulation** | `gpu-h100-sxm` | 80GB | 8.95 | 537 | MPS needs large VRAM for bond dimension |
| **Fast end-to-end pipeline** | `gpu-h100-2x` | 160GB | 17.47 | 1,048 | 2× H100 for parallel eval |
| **Budget development/debugging** | `gpu-rtx-4090` | 24GB | 1.45 | 87 | Cheapest GPU, good for code testing |
| **Maximum scale (40q state-vector)** | `gpu-h100-8x` | 640GB | 66.50 | 3,990 | Full 40q SV needs ~80GB, 8x for headroom |
| **Best $/performance** | `gpu-gh200` | 96GB | 4.78 | 287 | Grace Hopper — 1.2-1.9× faster than H100 |

|---|---|---|---|---|
| Clone repo + install deps | `gpu-l40s` | ~15 | ~4 min | Environment ready |

| GQE baseline (CUDA-Q solvers.gqe) | `gpu-l40s` | ~200 | ~53 min | Baseline energies |
| 40q MPS scaling benchmark | `gpu-h100-sxm` | ~500 | ~1h | Scaling plot data |
| H2 (4q) on Rigetti Cepheus QPU | QPU | ~53 | 1 batch task | Real hardware energy |
| LiH (8q) on Rigetti (batch, 100 shots) | QPU | ~819 | 1 batch task | 8q on real hardware |
| Free simulator validation (all molecules) | `qbraid:sim:qir-sv` | **0** | — | Reproducibility proof |
| **Total** | | **~3,567** | | **Leaves ~7,433 for iteration** |

## Step 3: Set Up the Environment on qBraid Lab

Once your GPU instance is running (accessible via JupyterLab, VS Code, or Terminal):

```bash
# Clone your repo
git clone https://github.com/Quantum-Buddies/Conditional_GQE.git
cd Conditional_GQE

# Install all dependencies (CUDA-Q + qBraid SDK + chemistry stack)
pip install -r requirements-qbraid.txt

# Set qBraid API key (found at https://account.qbraid.com/api-keys)
export QBRAID_API_KEY="your_api_key_here"

# Verify CUDA-Q is installed
python -c "import cudaq; print('CUDA-Q version:', cudaq.__version__)"

# Verify qBraid SDK
python -c "from qbraid import QbraidProvider; p=QbraidProvider(); print(len(p.get_devices()), 'devices available')"
```

## Step 4: Run the Full Pipeline on qBraid GPU

### Option A: Full automated pipeline

```bash
bash scripts/run_gic2026_scaling.sh
```

### Option B: Step-by-step (more control)

```bash
# 1. Generate Hamiltonians for all 33 molecules
python src/gqe/data/generate_hamiltonians.py \
    --config configs/experiment_scaling_gic2026.yaml \
    --out-dir results/data

# 2. RL training (DAPO with UCCSD pool, curriculum learning)
python src/gqe/models/train_rl_dapo.py \
    --from-scratch \
    --hamiltonians results/data/hamiltonians_gic2026/hamiltonians.json \
    --molecules h2_0.74 lih_1.6_full n2_1.1_full beh2_1.3_full \
    --out results/train/h_cgqe_rl.pt \
    --epochs 300 --lr 3e-4 --n-samples 50 \
    --use-bf16 --curriculum --curriculum-warmup 30 \
    --explore-eps 0.3 --adaptive-eps --top-p 0.9 \
    --force-entanglement

# 3. Inference — generate circuits from trained model
python src/gqe/models/infer_h_cgqe.py \
    --checkpoint results/train/h_cgqe_rl.pt \
    --hamiltonians results/data/hamiltonians_gic2026/hamiltonians.json \
    --out results/inference/h_cgqe_generated.json

# 4. Stage 2 — optimize rotation coefficients (L-BFGS-B)
python src/gqe/eval/optimize_h_cgqe_coefficients.py \
    --generated results/inference/h_cgqe_generated.json \
    --hamiltonians results/data/hamiltonians_gic2026/hamiltonians.json \
    --out results/eval/h_cgqe_optimized.json

# 5. Evaluate energies
python src/gqe/eval/evaluate_h_cgqe.py \
    --generated results/inference/h_cgqe_generated.json \
    --hamiltonians results/data/hamiltonians_gic2026/hamiltonians.json \
    --out results/eval/h_cgqe_evaluation.json
```

---

## Step 5: Run on Real QPU Hardware

### Method 1: CUDA-Q Native (zero code changes to your kernels)

This is the **recommended path** — your existing CUDA-Q Python kernels run on qBraid QPUs without any modification:

```python
import cudaq

# Set qBraid as the target — defaults to FREE state-vector simulator
cudaq.set_target("qbraid")

# Or target a REAL QPU:
cudaq.set_target("qbraid", machine="aws:rigetti:qpu:cepheus-1-108q")

# Your existing kernel code runs unchanged:
# result = cudaq.observe(kernel, hamiltonian, ...)
# energy = result.expectation()
```

For C++ kernels:
```bash
nvq++ --target qbraid --qbraid-machine "aws:rigetti:qpu:cepheus-1-108q" kernel.cpp -o kernel
export QBRAID_API_KEY="your_key"
./kernel
```

### Method 2: qBraid SDK (Qiskit circuit translation)

Your existing [qbraid_backend.py](file:///scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials/src/gqe/eval/qbraid_backend.py:0:0-0:0) already uses this approach:

```python
from qbraid import QbraidProvider
from qiskit import QuantumCircuit

provider = QbraidProvider()
device = provider.get_device("aws:rigetti:qpu:cepheus-1-108q")

# Submit a batch of measurement circuits (one per Hamiltonian term)
# This is ONE QPU task, not 15 separate tasks
job = device.run([meas_circ_1, meas_circ_2, ...], shots=1024, as_batch=True)
result = job.result()  # BatchResult with per-circuit counts

for i, circuit_result in enumerate(result.results):
    counts = circuit_result.data.get_counts()
    # compute expectation from counts
```

### QPU Cost Comparison (Batch vs Individual)

| Molecule | Qubits | Hamiltonian Terms | QPU | Individual (per-term) | Batch (1 task) | **Savings** |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| H2 | 4 | 15 | Rigetti Cepheus | 15×30 + 15×1024×0.0425 = 1,083 cr | 1×30 + 15×1024×0.0425 = 683 cr | **400 cr** |
| LiH | 8 | 185 | Rigetti Cepheus | 185×30 + 185×100×0.0425 = 6,286 cr | 1×30 + 185×100×0.0425 = 816 cr | **5,470 cr** |
| BeH2 | 14 | 731 | Rigetti Cepheus | 731×30 + 731×100×0.0425 = 24,914 cr | 1×30 + 731×100×0.0425 = 3,137 cr | **21,777 cr** |
| H2 | 4 | 15 | IQM Garnet | 15×30 + 15×1024×0.145 = 2,677 cr | 1×30 + 15×1024×0.145 = 2,227 cr | 450 cr |
| H2 | 4 | 15 | IonQ Forte-1 | 15×30 + 15×1024×8 = 123,780 cr | 1×30 + 15×1024×8 = 122,910 cr | 870 cr |

**Always use batch mode (`as_batch=True`)** — it's dramatically cheaper for multi-term Hamiltonians.

### Recommended QPU Execution Plan

| Priority | Molecule | Qubits | QPU | Shots | Est. Cost (batch) | Why |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| **1** | H2 | 4 | Rigetti Cepheus-1-108Q | 1024 | ~683 cr | Cheapest real hardware result |
| **2** | LiH | 8 | Rigetti Cepheus-1-108Q | 100 | ~816 cr | 8q on real hardware |
| **3** | H2 | 4 | IonQ Forte-1 | 1024 | ~122,910 cr | ❌ Too expensive, skip |
| **4** | All molecules | 4-20 | `qbraid:sim:qir-sv` | 2000 | **0 cr** | Free validation for judges |

---

## Step 6: Free Simulator Validation (Zero Credits)

Use qBraid's free QIR state-vector simulator for all molecules up to 30 qubits:

```python
import cudaq

# Free simulator — no credits charged
cudaq.set_target("qbraid", machine="qbraid:qbraid:sim:qir-sv")

# Run all your molecules (H2=4q, LiH=8q, BeH2=14q, N2=20q — all < 30q)
# Same kernel code, same results, zero cost
result = cudaq.observe(kernel, hamiltonian, n_qubits, n_electrons, pauli_words, thetas)
energy = result.expectation()
```

This is what **judges will run** to verify your results. Include a script `scripts/validate_on_qbraid.py`:

```bash
# Judges run this — costs nothing
export QBRAID_API_KEY="their_key"
python scripts/validate_on_qbraid.py \
    --hamiltonians results/data/hamiltonians.json \
    --optimized results/eval/h_cgqe_optimized.json
```

## Step 7: Save Results and Stop Instance

**Critical**: On-demand instances don't auto-sync files. You must push results back to GitHub before terminating:

```bash
# Commit all results to git (LFS handles large files)
git add results/
git commit -m "results: qBraid GPU pipeline run + QPU energies for H2 and LiH"
git push origin main

# THEN stop or terminate the instance
# Stop = preserves disk, ~0.02 cr/min storage only
# Terminate = deletes everything, $0
```

## Complete Workflow Summary

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│  YOUR LOCAL AIRE HPC                                            │
│  - Develop & debug code                                         │

│  - docs/QBRAID_STRATEGY.md                                      │
│  - requirements-qbraid.txt                                      │
└──────────────────────────┬──────────────────────────────────────┘
                           │ git clone
                           ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐

│  - Run full pipeline: bash scripts/run_gic2026_scaling.sh      │
│  - CUDA-Q GPU simulation (nvidia target, same as AIRE)         │

└──────────┬──────────────────────────────┬───────────────────────┘
           │                              │
           ▼                              ▼
┌─────────────────────────┐  ┌───────────────────────────────────┐
│  QBRAID FREE SIMULATOR  │  │  QBRAID QPU (real hardware)       │
│  qbraid:sim:qir-sv      │  │  - Rigetti Cepheus-1-108Q         │

│  - Up to 30 qubits      │  │  - H2: ~683 cr (batch)            │
│  - Judges run this      │  │  - LiH: ~816 cr (batch)           │
│  - All molecules        │  │  - CUDA-Q: set_target("qbraid",   │
│    validated here       │  │    machine="aws:rigetti:...")     │
└─────────────────────────┘  └───────────────────────────────────┘
```

### Two Ways to Access QPUs from Your Code

| Feature | CUDA-Q Native (`cudaq.set_target`) | qBraid SDK (`QbraidProvider`) |
|---|---|---|
| **Code changes needed** | None — just change `set_target()` | Translate to Qiskit circuit first |
| **Your existing code** | Works as-is | Needs [qbraid_backend.py](file:///scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials/src/gqe/eval/qbraid_backend.py:0:0-0:0) translation |
| **Batch mode** | Automatic (single `observe` call) | `as_batch=True` parameter |
| **Async/future persistence** | `cudaq.sample_async` + save to disk | `job.async_result(timeout=120)` |
| **C++ support** | `nvq++ --target qbraid` | Python only |
| **Best for** | Our CUDA-Q kernels in [train_rl_dapo.py](file:///scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials/src/gqe/models/train_rl_dapo.py:0:0-0:0) | Qiskit circuits in [qbraid_backend.py](file:///scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials/src/gqe/eval/qbraid_backend.py:0:0-0:0) |

### Key Reminders

- **Always use batch mode** for multi-term Hamiltonian measurements — saves 90%+ on per-task fees
- **Stop (don't terminate)** if you want to resume work later — preserves disk at ~0.02 cr/min
- **Push to GitHub before terminating** — on-demand disks are deleted on terminate
- **Free simulator for judges** — include a `validate_on_qbraid.py` script that costs 0 credits
- **CUDA-Q native path is preferred** — your kernels already work, just swap `set_target("nvidia")` → `set_target("qbraid", machine="aws:rigetti:qpu:cepheus-1-108q")`

Want me to update the [QBRAID_STRATEGY.md](file:///scratch/kcwp264/Conditional-GQE_materials/docs/QBRAID_STRATEGY.md:0:0-0:0) in the repo with all this info and push it?
